          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript.


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concierto with each other in specifico
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is base on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantl
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating, groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal,
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

        output: >-
          Genes work together in harmony to carry out their functions, and
          understanding how these genes contribute to complex traits requires a
          deeper insight into gene expression regulation across various
          conditions. Transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on
          the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms; however,
          modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role
          in disease origin and progression. In this study, we introduce
          PhenoPLIER – a novel computational approach that maps gene-trait
          associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common
          latent representation for joint analysis. Based on modules of genes
          with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, we
          observe significant association between diseases and gene modules
          expressed in relevant cell types. Moreover, our method accurately
          predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. 


          Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that
          functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized
          by PhenoPLIER within trait-associated gene modules. In conclusion,
          PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations through groups of
          co-expressed genes, revealing potential targets missed by single-gene
          strategies. This novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          offers a unique perspective on gene module-based drug repurposing.

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concierto with each other in specifico
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is base on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantl
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating, groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal,
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

      response:
        output: >-
          In the study of complex traits, genes act in concert with each other
          under specific contexts to perform their functions. To gain a
          mechanistic understanding of gene expression regulation across various
          conditions is essential for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped identify the role
          of individual genes in disease mechanisms; however, modern models
          predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease
          origins and progression.


          PhenoPLIER, a novel computational approach introduced here, maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for joint analysis. By incorporating
          modules of co-expressed genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions, we observed that diseases are significantly
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types and
          accurately predict known drug-disease pairs as well as mechanisms of
          action. Additionally, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid
          regulation, we identified functionally important players lacking
          associations but prioritized within trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER.


          In conclusion, PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations through
          the lens of gene modules and reveals potential targets missed by
          single-gene strategies. This novel framework provides a comprehensive
          approach for interpreting genetic associations and drug repurposing
          opportunities in complex diseases, advancing our understanding of
          disease etiology and therapeutic mechanisms.

      response:
        output: >-
          Gene interactions play a crucial role in complex trait etiology, and
          understanding their influence requires a mechanistic examination of
          gene expression across different conditions. Transcriptome-wide
          association studies have shed light on the individual roles of genes
          in disease mechanisms; however, modern models predict that gene-gene
          interactions are key to disease origin and progression. To address
          this, we present PhenoPLIER – a computational approach mapping
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for joint analysis. Built upon modules of
          co-expressed genes across relevant cell types, our method accurately
          predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action.
          Additionally, through CRISPR screening of lipid regulation, we
          identify functionally important players lacking associations but
          prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. Overall,
          integrating groups of co-expressed genes with PhenoPLIER offers a
          novel framework for interpreting genetic associations and uncovers
          potential targets overlooked by single-gene strategies, offering
          insights into therapeutic targeting and drug repurposing.

          Genetic studies aim to uncover the molecular mechanisms underlying
          complex traits, while functional genomics seeks to understand gene
          expression patterns across various conditions. Despite significant
          advances in transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS), a
          comprehensive understanding of genetic associations remains elusive
          due to limited mechanistic insights and insufficient coverage of gene
          interactions. The current gap is particularly evident when it comes to
          identifying potential therapeutic targets through the repurposing of
          existing drugs, as single-gene approaches often overlook crucial
          co-expression patterns among genes in complex traits.


          In this study, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach
          designed to map gene-trait associations and pharmacological
          perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint
          analysis, by leveraging modules of genes with similar expression
          patterns across the same conditions. By incorporating groups of
          co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations
          and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. Our
          findings show that diseases are significantly associated with gene
          modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is accurate
          in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of
          action. Additionally, we observed functional importance players lack
          associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation.


          In conclusion, the application of PhenoPLIER represents an innovative
          framework for interpreting genetic associations through gene module
          analysis and holds potential implications not only in drug repurposing
          but also in elucidating disease etiology and uncovering novel
          therapeutic targets.

          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations

          innovative approach, PhenoPLIER, for projecting genetic associations
          through gene expression patterns. By integrating transcriptome-wide
          association studies with pharmacological perturbation data and
          clustering complex traits based on co-expressed genes, this method can
          provide a mechanistic understanding of disease etiology and drug
          mechanisms.


          The context is the critical need to bridge the gap between single-gene
          strategies and functional genomics approaches for interpreting genetic
          associations in complex diseases. Our approach reveals potential
          targets missed by traditional methods while simultaneously providing
          insights into how these genes influence traits across diverse
          contexts.

          prediction of known drug-disease pairs and helps infer mechanisms of
          action in diseases by identifying gene modules that are significantly
          associated with specific traits.


          Our results demonstrate that this approach can contextualize genetic

          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on

          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          mechanisms, thus advancing functional genomics research and

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach
          that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation
          data into a common latent representation for a joint analysis. Genes
          act in concert with each other in specific contexts to perform their
          functions. Determining how these genes influence complex traits
          requires a mechanistic understanding of expression regulation across
          different conditions. It has been shown that this insight is critical
          for developing new therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies
          have helped uncover the role of individual genes in disease-relevant
          mechanisms. However, modern models of the architecture of complex
          traits predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in
          disease origin and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based
          on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same
          conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly associated with
          gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is
          accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring
          mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze
          lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
          associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

          Projecting genetic associations through gene expression patterns
          highlights disease etiology and drug mechanisms. We present
          PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations
          and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent
          representation for joint analysis. By determining how genes influence
          complex traits with mechanistic understanding of expression regulation
          across different conditions, we provide a novel framework for
          interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.
          Our method reveals potential targets missed by single-gene strategies

          Furthermore, we validate the importance of PhenoPLIER's approach by
          finding that functionally important players lack associations but are
          prioritized in trait-associated modules through a CRISPR screen on
          lipid regulation. In conclusion, our study fills an essential gap in
          understanding genetic studies by projecting gene expression patterns
          and unveiling disease etiology and drug mechanisms using functional
          genomics.

          We introduce PhenoPLIER, a novel computational approach designed to
          bridge the gap between genetic studies and functional genomics. By
          mapping gene-trait associations with pharmacological perturbation data
          into a common latent representation for joint analysis, we uncover the
          underlying mechanisms responsible for complex trait development.
          Recognizing that genes act in concert with each other to perform their
          functions, our focus is on understanding how these co-expressed genes
          influence disease etiology and potential therapeutic targets. 


          Transcriptome-wide association studies have proven valuable in
          identifying individual gene roles within disease processes; however,
          modern models of complex traits predict a significant role for
          gene-gene interactions in origin and progression. To achieve this
          mechanistic insight, PhenoPLIER identifies modules of genes with
          similar expression patterns across specific conditions, allowing us to
          observe associations between diseases and these co-expressed genetic
          elements. Our approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease
          pairs and inferring mechanisms of action through the identification of
          functionally important players within trait-associated gene modules.


          To further validate PhenoPLIER's potential applications, we conducted
          a CRISPR screen on lipid regulation, revealing that key functional
          components lacking associations are prioritized by PhenoPLIER in
          trait-associated modules. Thus, our method contextualizes genetic
          associations and uncovers potential targets missed by single-gene
          strategies, ultimately providing insights into disease etiology and
          drug repurposing. 


          In conclusion, PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting
          genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, enriching
          understanding of complex trait development and facilitating targeted
          therapeutic interventions.

          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations

          association studies, our approach aims to provide a comprehensive

          associations and uncover novel therapeutic targets that may have been
          overlooked through traditional single-gene strategies. This approach
          has significant implications for drug repurposing efforts and can

          associated with various diseases.

          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          PhenoPLIER: A Computational Approach for Projecting Genetic
          Associations and Revealing Therapeutic Mechanisms


          Background: To fill the gap in understanding gene-gene interactions,
          we propose PhenoPLIER - a computational method that maps genetic
          associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common
          latent representation for joint analysis. This approach allows us to
          study complex traits by considering how genes act in concert with each
          other under different conditions.

          efforts.

          genetic associations and uncover undiscovered therapeutic mechanisms
          by integrating groups of co-expressed genes. This approach has broad
          implications for understanding disease etiology and developing new
          therapies in the field of functional genomics.

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concert with each other in specific
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies. Our
          results in this study show that diseases are significantly associated
          with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach
          is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring
          mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze
          lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
          associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in

          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players

          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

        raw: >-
          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concert with each other in specific
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER.

          In the context of genetic studies and functional genomics, this
          manuscript addresses a gap by proposing an innovative approach to gene
          co-expression analysis in complex traits. Our study aims to uncover
          disease etiology and drug mechanisms through projecting gene
          expression patterns. The content section presents PhenoPLIER, a
          computational method that integrates transcriptome data with
          pharmacological perturbation information, enabling the mapping of
          trait associations onto common latent representations derived from
          modules exhibiting similar expression profiles across different
          conditions. We demonstrate its accuracy in predicting known
          drug-disease pairs, inferring mechanisms of action and identifying
          functionally important players lacking associations but prioritized

          perspective on disease etiology and suggest potential avenues for drug
          repurposing by leveraging gene co-expression patterns.

          Genes collaborate in the performance of their functions, and a deeper
          understanding of gene expression regulation across different contexts
          is essential for deciphering complex traits. Transcriptome-wide
          association studies have shed light on individual genes' roles in
          disease mechanisms; however, modern approaches to the study of complex

          PhenoPLIER. These findings highlight the potential for gene
          co-expression patterns as a powerful tool for identifying therapeutic
          targets and drug repurposing strategies in complex diseases.

        - raw: >-
            You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
            improving the text of a manuscript with title '{{ title }}' and
            keywords '{{ keywords }}.' Revise the following abstract of this
            manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and follows a
            context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a single
            paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary to
            follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
            follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to
            the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients
            the reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
            research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands
            on the open question that the research answered. A successful
            context section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s
            contributions from the current state of the art by communicating
            what is missing in the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why
            that matters (i.e., the connection between the specific gap and the
            broader context that the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here
            we") first describes the novel method or approach that you used to
            fill the gap or question. Then you present the meat—your executive
            summary of the results; 3) Finally, the conclusion interprets the
            results to answer the question that was posed at the end of the
            context section. There is often a second part to the conclusion
            section that highlights how this conclusion moves the broader field
            forward (i.e., "broader significance").

              ---

              Genes work together in harmony to carry out their functions, and
              understanding how these genes contribute to complex traits
              requires a deeper insight into gene expression regulation across
              various conditions. Transcriptome-wide association studies have
              shed light on the role of individual genes in disease-related
              mechanisms; however, modern models predict that gene-gene
              interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and
              progression. In this study, we introduce PhenoPLIER – a novel
              computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and
              pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent
              representation for joint analysis. Based on modules of genes with
              similar expression patterns across the same conditions, we observe
              significant association between diseases and gene modules
              expressed in relevant cell types. Moreover, our method accurately
              predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of
              action. 


              Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that
              functionally important players lack associations but are
              prioritized by PhenoPLIER within trait-associated gene modules. In
              conclusion, PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations through
              groups of co-expressed genes, revealing potential targets missed
              by single-gene strategies. This novel framework for interpreting
              genetic associations offers a unique perspective on gene
              module-based drug repurposing.

              ---

              Genetic studies aim to uncover the molecular mechanisms underlying
              complex traits, while functional genomics seeks to understand gene
              expression patterns across various conditions. Despite significant
              advances in transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS), a
              comprehensive understanding of genetic associations remains
              elusive due to limited mechanistic insights and insufficient
              coverage of gene interactions. The current gap is particularly
              evident when it comes to identifying potential therapeutic targets
              through the repurposing of existing drugs, as single-gene
              approaches often overlook crucial co-expression patterns among
              genes in complex traits.


              In this study, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach
              designed to map gene-trait associations and pharmacological
              perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint
              analysis, by leveraging modules of genes with similar expression
              patterns across the same conditions. By incorporating groups of
              co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic
              associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene
              strategies. Our findings show that diseases are significantly
              associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and
              our approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs
              and inferring mechanisms of action. Additionally, we observed
              functional importance players lack associations but are
              prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER using a
              CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation.


              In conclusion, the application of PhenoPLIER represents an
              innovative framework for interpreting genetic associations through
              gene module analysis and holds potential implications not only in
              drug repurposing but also in elucidating disease etiology and
              uncovering novel therapeutic targets.

              using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that
              functionally important players lack associations but are
              prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In
              conclusion, by incorporating, groups of co-expressed genes,
              PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal,
              potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
              provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
              through the lens of gene modules.

              This study aims to address a gap in the literature by presenting
              an innovative approach, PhenoPLIER, for projecting genetic
              associations through gene expression patterns. By integrating
              transcriptome-wide association studies with pharmacological
              perturbation data and clustering complex traits based on
              co-expressed genes, this method can provide a mechanistic
              understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.


              The context is the critical need to bridge the gap between
              single-gene strategies and functional genomics approaches for
              interpreting genetic associations in complex diseases. Our
              approach reveals potential targets missed by traditional methods
              while simultaneously providing insights into how these genes
              influence traits across diverse contexts.


              PhenoPLIER maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological
              perturbation data onto a common latent representation of
              co-expressed genes, which is derived from modules exhibiting
              similar expression patterns within the same conditions. This
              method offers an accurate prediction of known drug-disease pairs
              and helps infer mechanisms of action in diseases by identifying
              gene modules that are significantly associated with specific
              traits.


              Our results demonstrate that this approach can contextualize
              genetic associations and reveal potential therapeutic targets,
              such as functionally important players lacking associations but
              prioritized in trait-associated modules through PhenoPLIER's
              CRISPR screen analysis on lipid regulation. In conclusion, by
              incorporating gene co-expression patterns, the novel framework
              provided by PhenoPLIER enables a more comprehensive understanding
              of genetic determinants and facilitates drug repurposing
              strategies for complex diseases.

              ---

              In the field of genetic studies, understanding how genes
              collaborate in specific contexts to perform their functions is
              crucial for deciphering complex traits. Current models predict
              that gene-gene interactions play a central role in disease
              etiology and progression. However, transcriptome-wide association
              studies have only begun to uncover individual gene roles, leaving
              a significant gap in our knowledge of the mechanistic
              underpinnings of expression regulation across diverse conditions.


              Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER—a computational approach that maps
              gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into
              a common latent representation for joint analysis. By modeling
              genes with similar expression patterns as modules, PhenoPLIER
              identifies disease-associated gene modules expressed in relevant
              cell types, accurately predicting known drug-disease pairs and
              elucidating mechanisms of action. Additionally, we demonstrate its
              utility by using CRISPR screening to analyze lipid
              regulation—highlighting functionally important players that lack
              associations but are prioritized within trait-associated modules
              via PhenoPLIER's predictions.


              In conclusion, the incorporation of co-expressed gene groups
              through PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting
              genetic associations and contextualizing complex traits, revealing
              potential therapeutic targets missed by single-gene strategies.
              Our findings underscore the importance of analyzing genes in
              concert with one another to understand disease etiology and
              identify novel drug mechanisms, thus advancing functional genomics
              research and contributing to the field's broader scientific
              landscape.

              We introduce PhenoPLIER, a cutting-edge computational approach
              that harnesses gene expression patterns to map genetic
              associations and pharmacological perturbation data into an
              integrated latent representation for joint analysis. By
              recognizing the concerted actions of genes in specific contexts,
              our method sheds light on complex traits by elucidating their
              underlying mechanisms. This insight is crucial for devising novel
              therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped
              identify individual gene roles in disease processes; however,
              modern models emphasize that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal
              role in the origin and progression of diseases.


              PhenoPLIER represents genes as modules with similar expression
              patterns across relevant cellular contexts. We observe significant
              associations between various diseases and specific gene modules,
              which validate our predictive accuracy for predicting known
              drug-disease pairs and elucidating their underlying mechanisms.
              Additionally, analyzing lipid regulation using a CRISPR screen, we
              find that functionally important players are prioritized in
              trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER but lack associations
              themselves.


              In conclusion, PhenoPLIER offers novel strategies to contextualize
              genetic associations by integrating gene co-expression patterns
              and unveils potential targets missed by single-gene approaches.
              This innovative framework allows for a comprehensive understanding
              of complex traits through the lens of gene modules.

              ---

              We introduce PhenoPLIER, a novel computational approach designed
              to bridge the gap between genetic studies and functional genomics.
              By mapping gene-trait associations with pharmacological
              perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint
              analysis, we uncover the underlying mechanisms responsible for
              complex trait development. Recognizing that genes act in concert
              with each other to perform their functions, our focus is on
              understanding how these co-expressed genes influence disease
              etiology and potential therapeutic targets. 


              Transcriptome-wide association studies have proven valuable in
              identifying individual gene roles within disease processes;
              however, modern models of complex traits predict a significant
              role for gene-gene interactions in origin and progression. To
              achieve this mechanistic insight, PhenoPLIER identifies modules of
              genes with similar expression patterns across specific conditions,
              allowing us to observe associations between diseases and these
              co-expressed genetic elements. Our approach is accurate in
              predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of
              action through the identification of functionally important
              players within trait-associated gene modules.


              To further validate PhenoPLIER's potential applications, we
              conducted a CRISPR screen on lipid regulation, revealing that key
              functional components lacking associations are prioritized by
              PhenoPLIER in trait-associated modules. Thus, our method
              contextualizes genetic associations and uncovers potential targets
              missed by single-gene strategies, ultimately providing insights
              into disease etiology and drug repurposing. 


              In conclusion, PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for
              interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene
              modules, enriching understanding of complex trait development and
              facilitating targeted therapeutic interventions.

              ---

              In this manuscript, we present PhenoPLIER, a cutting-edge
              computational tool designed to analyze gene expression patterns
              and elucidate the intricate relationships between complex traits,
              genetic associations, and drug mechanisms. By harnessing
              transcriptome-wide association studies, our approach aims to
              provide a comprehensive understanding of how gene functions in
              concert with other genes within specific contexts, ultimately
              contributing to disease etiology and therapeutic target
              identification for various diseases. 


              PhenoPLIER's primary strength lies in its ability to identify
              groups of co-expressed genes that have been correlated with
              complex traits by leveraging modules of similar expression
              patterns across relevant cell types. This strategy enables us to
              better predict known drug-disease associations, as well as infer
              the mechanisms behind these relationships. Additionally, we use a
              CRISPR screen for lipid regulation, demonstrating how PhenoPLIER
              effectively identifies functional players and prioritizes them
              within trait-associated gene modules.


              In summary, by incorporating co-expressed genes into our analysis,
              PhenoPLIER offers an innovative framework to interpret genetic
              associations and uncover novel therapeutic targets that may have

              ---

              PhenoPLIER: A Computational Approach for Projecting Genetic
              Associations and Revealing Therapeutic Mechanisms


              Background: To fill the gap in understanding gene-gene
              interactions, we propose PhenoPLIER - a computational method that
              maps genetic associations and pharmacological perturbation data
              into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This
              approach allows us to study complex traits by considering how
              genes act in concert with each other under different conditions. 


              Method: By analyzing transcriptome-wide association studies, we
              demonstrate the importance of understanding gene expression
              regulation in disease etiology and drug mechanisms. PhenoPLIER
              identifies modules of co-expressed genes across relevant cell
              types and uses them to predict known drug-disease pairs and infer
              their therapeutic mechanisms. We further validate our approach
              using a CRISPR screen on lipid regulation.


              Results: Our findings reveal that diseases are significantly
              associated with gene expression patterns in specific cell types,
              validating the utility of our method for identifying potential
              therapeutic targets. Additionally, we find functional players
              prioritized in trait-associated modules but lacking associations,
              suggesting they could be valuable candidates for drug repurposing
              efforts. 


              Conclusion: PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework to contextualize
              genetic associations and uncover undiscovered therapeutic
              mechanisms by integrating groups of co-expressed genes. This
              approach has broad implications for understanding disease etiology
              and developing new therapies in the field of functional genomics.

              in a single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely
              necessary to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the
              abstract must follow the following structure: 1) The context must
              communicate to the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first
              sentence orients the reader by introducing the broader field in
              which the particular research is situated. Then, this context is
              narrowed until it lands on the open question that the research
              answered. A successful context section sets the stage for
              distinguishing the paper’s contributions from the current state of
              the art by communicating what is missing in the literature (i.e.,
              the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e., the connection
              between the specific gap and the broader context that the paper
              opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the novel
              method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question. Then
              you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
              Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the
              question that was posed at the end of the context section. There
              is often a second part to the conclusion section that highlights
              how this conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e.,
              "broader significance").


              Input paragraph: We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach
              that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation
              data into a common latent representation for a joint analysis.

              perform their functions. Determining how these genes influence

              regulation across different conditions. It has been shown that

              modern models of the architecture of complex traits predict that

              analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important
              players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated
              modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of
              co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic
              associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene
              strategies. PhenoPLIER provides a novel framework for interpreting
              genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.

              ---

              We present PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates
              gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into
              a common latent representation for joint analysis. Genes exhibit
              concerted action with each other in specific contexts to perform
              their functions, necessitating a mechanistic understanding of
              expression regulation across diverse conditions. This insight is
              crucial for the development of novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide
              association studies have aided in identifying individual gene
              roles in disease-related mechanisms; however, modern models
              suggest that gene-gene interactions play an essential role in
              disease origin and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation relies
              on modules comprising genes with similar expression patterns
              across shared conditions. We find a strong correlation between
              diseases and expressed gene modules in relevant cell types, as
              well as accurate predictions of known drug-disease pairs and
              inferred mechanisms of action. A CRISPR screen for lipid
              regulation further validates our approach by revealing
              functionally significant players that lack associations but are
              prioritized within trait-associated modules using PhenoPLIER. In
              conclusion, incorporating groups of co-expressed genes allows
              PhenoPLIER to contextualize genetic associations and unearth
              potential targets missed through single-gene strategies. By
              providing a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
              via gene modules, PhenoPLIER enhances our understanding of complex
              traits and therapeutic mechanisms.

              analysis of known drug-disease pairs, revealing novel mechanistic
              insights into therapeutic targets. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen
              to study lipid regulation, we highlight the prioritization of
              functionally important players in trait-associated modules by
              PhenoPLIER - demonstrating its potential for gene co-expression
              clustering and drug repurposing. In conclusion, our work
              highlights the importance of incorporating groups of co-expressed
              genes through PhenoPLIER to contextualize genetic associations and
              uncover untapped therapeutic potentials missed by single-gene
              strategies. By providing a novel framework for interpreting
              genetic associations, PhenoPLIER advances functional genomics
              research in drug repurposing and therapeutic target discovery.

              Genes function in harmony, orchestrating complex processes. To
              fully comprehend their roles in determining intricate traits, an
              understanding of gene expression regulation across various
              contexts is essential. This knowledge has proven vital for
              advancing therapeutic advancements by identifying the functional
              role individual genes play in disease mechanisms. However, modern

              significantly to disease etiology and progression. Herein, we
              introduce PhenoPLIER – a novel computational approach designed to
              map gene-trait associations with pharmacological perturbation data
              into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. Based on
              modules of genes displaying similar expression patterns across
              identical conditions, this method enables the identification of

              accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs, elucidating
              mechanisms of action. Furthermore, utilizing CRISPR screening to
              examine lipid regulation, we discovered functionally important
              players lacking associations but prioritized in trait-associated

              ---

              Genes collaborate in concert to execute their functions within
              specific contexts. Understanding the intricate interplay of gene
              expression and its regulation is essential for deciphering complex
              traits and identifying novel therapeutic targets. Though
              transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on
              individual genes' roles in disease mechanisms, recent advances in
              genomics indicate that gene-gene interactions hold crucial
              importance in determining the origin and progression of diseases.


              We introduce PhenoPLIER - a computational approach for mapping
              associations between genetic traits and pharmacological
              perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint
              analysis. This innovative method leverages modules of genes
              displaying similar expression patterns across relevant cell types,
              enabling us to project gene-trait relationships with enhanced
              accuracy. We demonstrate that diseases are significantly
              associated with modules expressed in disease-relevant cellular
              contexts, validating our approach's ability to predict known
              drug-disease associations and elucidate their underlying
              mechanisms.


              Furthermore, utilizing a CRISPR screen for lipid regulation, we
              reveal that functionally important players do not exhibit strong
              association signals but still emerge as prioritized
              traits-associated modules identified by PhenoPLIER. These findings
              highlight the potential of our method to identify novel
              therapeutic targets and support drug repurposing strategies in
              diverse disease contexts.

              approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological
              perturbation data into a common latent representation for a joint
              analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with
              similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We observe
              that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules
              expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is accurate in
              predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of
              action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid
              regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
              associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
              PhenoPLIER.

              Our study addresses the gap in understanding how complex traits
              are regulated by gene-gene interactions. We fill this gap by
              introducing PhenoPLIER, a novel computational approach that maps
              gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into
              a common latent representation for joint analysis. This approach

              abstract must follow the following structure: 1) The context must
              communicate to the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first
              sentence orients the reader by introducing the broader field in
              which the particular research is situated. Then, this context is
              narrowed until it lands on the open question that the research
              answered. A successful context section sets the stage for
              distinguishing the paper’s contributions from the current state of
              the art by communicating what is missing in the literature (i.e.,
              the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e., the connection
              between the specific gap and the broader context that the paper

              question that was posed at the end of the context section. There
              is often a second part to the conclusion section that highlights
              how this conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e.,

            text: >-
              This research addresses the critical need for a mechanistic
              understanding of gene expression regulation in complex traits,
              which is essential for developing novel therapies. While
              transcriptome-wide association studies have identified individual
              genes' role in disease mechanisms, modern models predict that
              gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease origin
              and progression. Here, we present PhenoPLIER - a novel
              computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and
              pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent
              representation for joint analysis. By identifying modules of genes
              with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, our
              method reveals significant associations between diseases and
              specific gene modules expressed in relevant cell types.
              Furthermore, our findings accurately predict known drug-disease
              pairs and infer mechanisms of action, highlighting potential
              therapeutic targets. Using CRISPR screening to analyze lipid
              regulation, we find that functionally important players are
              prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER despite
              lacking associations. Overall, this work fills an essential gap in
              the literature on functional genomics and advances our
              understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.
